Tracking Initiative

Until you get the hang out it, it might be difficult to keep track of when exactly things are happening, which is why we've provided the combat strip on the Basic Combat Cheat Sheet, which is that numbered line on the left hand side going 1 to 20.

To use it, take a marker (dice or a penny works just fine) and place it on your initiative. When that number comes up on the Clock, you do your action and then add the seconds until your next action, looping back around to 0, if needed.

So, if you attack on second 17 with a weapon with a recovery of 8, place a marker on 17. When the Clock gets to second 17, you attack, and then move the marker to 5 ( since 17 + 8 is above 20, you subtract 20 from it).

You may also find it useful to place markers for other things you want to track, like movement and shooting for ranged fighters or when spells will go off or end.

IMPROVISED WEAPONS

It is entirely possible that you’ll find yourself unarmed and must defend yourself with a chair leg or broken bottle. In these cases, treat is as a club but penalize it for 3 seconds and drop its Parry to 0 for not being balanced properly.

Now, it’s also possible that you have superhuman strength and so you can rip a door off its hinges and start bashing people with want and abandon. In this case, it’s entirely possible to do more than a zweihander in sheer concussive force, however, you’ll find that because a door (or similar) isn’t properly weighted or balanced, you’ll find yourself taking a long time to swing and unable to defend yourself properly. In extreme cases like these, the GM can give you a Defense Modifier of somewhere around -8 and the Recovery should never be quicker than 20 seconds.

On top of this, these wide swings make an otherwise threatening object easy to dodge and so less of a danger, meaning you may still be counted as unarmed while trying to use it.

WEAPON STYLES

The way your character wields a weapon can tell a lot about their priorities in a combat and a weapon can be versatile in the extreme if some thought is put into how it's held.

1 HANDED WEAPON WITH SHIELD This is the standard way your character is assumed to be wielding their weapon.

1 HANDED WEAPON WITH FREE SECOND HAND With one hand free, the character can focus a bit more on their defense and seizing openings. They gain a +2 to their Parry and -1 to their Recovery.

2 1 HANDED WEAPONS If attacking with both, your primary hand attacks with a d12! while your secondary attacks with a d8!. You only receive your main hand’s Parry, if any. The secondary attacks slightly slower; always attacking a number of seconds equal to its Recovery after the primary makes its attacks.

When defending with one hand, you defend against the first attack against you with a d20!, using the Parry value of the off-hand weapon.

You can only switch Tactics on your primary attack.

If the weapon off-hand weapon is as large as you, you can only attack with it.

2 1 HANDED WEAPONS (with Ambidextrous Mastery) f attacking with both, both attack with a d20!. You only receive your main hand’s Parry, if any. The secondary attacks slightly slower; always attacking a number of seconds equal to its Recovery after the primary makes its attacks.

When defending with one, you defend against the first attack against you with a d20!, using the full Parry of one and half the Parry of the other, added together (the combination is your choice).

You can only switch Tactics and Weapon Styles on your primary attack.

If the weapon off-hand is as large as you, you can only attack with it.

2 HANDING 1 HANDED WEAPONS Using two hands where one is sufficient allows you to leverage more of your strength and deal a harsher blow but slows you down.

When using this style, you increase your damage by +4, however, you swap out the bonuses for a +3 to Recovery.

This style can’t be effectively used in combat on weapons smaller than your size.

DOUBLE SHIELDS While it gives no offensive benefit, the double shields style allows you to cover more facings to gain a greater defense. If two shields have overlapping faces, use the higher of the two's Parry.

WEAPON IN YOUR OFF HAND This may come about because your main hand is disabled or you just want to show off. But this implies the main hand is free. If not, see the 2 1 Handed Weapons style.

Non-Ambidextrous people attack with a d12! And gain a +5 Recovery penalty to off-handed attacks.

2 HANDS FREE (NO WEAPON) If you have no weapons in combat, you’re not really seen as a threat and so an attacker doesn’t really have to do the dance to try to avoid you blows while trying to strike you.

In this style, your opponent attacks at half their weapon Recovery and they get a free attack whenever you try and close in an attack them. That includes moving ranges.

Dropping Your Weapon to Grapple

Unarmed moves are more effective with two hands rather than one so you may decide to drop your weapon to try and grapple your opponent easier. If the unarmed attack is on the same second that you drop your weapon, your opponent doesn’t receive the free attack on you.

The Combat

It is inevitable that if you get into combat, you’ll end up engaged in a melee with your enemy.

ZONES of ENGAGEMENT: MEASURING YOUR OPPONENT

There are 3 positions that you can have relative to your enemy. These positions aren’t reciprocal by nature: you can be threatened by someone and not have them be threatened by you.

NOT ENGAGED This is basically the default. You are too far away for them to reasonable do anything.

THREATENED This means that you’re within 5 feet + your opponent’s weapon’s measure. From here, if you move faster than a Jog and if they’re within half their weapon Recovery, they can attack you.

If you go from Engaged to Threatened, your weapon initiative doesn't reset, so if you become Engaged again for any reason before your weapon's Recovery, you may not attack.

ENGAGED You’ve closed the distance and can actually make an attack. The person with the highest weapon measure attacks first. If both weapons are exactly the same measure, the attacks happen simultaneously.

MELEE ATTACK & DEFENSE

Basic melee attacks always roll a base d20! + any attack modifiers, while defenders rolls a d20! + any defense modifiers

PERFECT ATTACKS & DEFENSES

A Perfect Attack or Defense is one that puts you in a better position to win. A Perfect Attack occurs on an unmodified roll of 20 on your Attack roll, while a Perfect Defense is an unmodified 20 on your Defense roll, but only if your final totals are still greater than you opponent’s. If not, the attack still hits for Perfect Attacks and you still dodge the blow for Perfect Defenses but you don’t gain the auxiliary benefits.

If both those qualifiers are met you gain the following benefits on top of the normal ones:

PERFECT ATTACK You gain a free automatically successful Unarmed Combat attack. If you have Advanced Training in Unarmed Combat, you may roll for the additional benefit, but even on a failure, you gain the move’s base benefit.

You may also Shift with no penalty and change your Tactic.

If you’re firing a ranged weapon, you don’t receive the Unarmed attack, however you managed to hit a sensitive area, lowering their max movement speed by one category, minimum Crawl speed.

PERFECT DEFENSE You gain a free automatically successful Unarmed Combat attack. If you have Advanced Training in Unarmed Combat, you may roll for the additional benefit, but even on a failure, you gain the move’s benefit.

You may also Shift with no penalty and change your Tactic.

Additionally, you gain a chance to riposte, gaining a free full attack the next second if you’re wielding a melee weapon. This attack doesn’t affect your current count.

NEAR PERFECT ATTACKS AND DEFENSE An unmodified roll of 19 on either Attack or Defense results in a Near Perfect Attack or Defense, where you may Shift for no penalty, change your Tactic, or, for attacks, gain an extra d4! to damage, or, for defenses gained a free riposte with your off hand weapon if it’s smaller than your size.

RANGED ATTACK & DEFENSE

Ranged attacks and defenses function identical to melee attacks and defenses (including Flanking), only get a few additional bonuses and penalties.

The first is that range (of course) and the second is movement. When defending against a ranged attack, you get a +2 for each Movement Category above standing still. So, if you're getting shot at, get the lead out.

COVER

Cover is anything that gets between you and the person trying to shoot at you.

For solid objects, such as walls, where it is easy to calculate how much of the person is covered, a flat bonus is appropriate. For every 10% covered, you get a +2, so 25% would be a +5, 50% would be a +10 and so on.

For people, tree, a moving object, or really anything that’s harder to calculate, you’ll use a dice backed on the difference in size, starting at a d20. Each difference in size shrinks or increases that cover die by 1 size.

Cover dice for most objects works the same as shields - if you’re behind cover, you add a roll of that dice to your defense. If you’re actively crouching behind it, you add the max it would give you to your defense plus another roll of the dice.

So, two medium size people standing one in front of the other would give the guy in the back d20 cover, same as two small sized people. If the person in the front is small sized and the guy in the back is still medium sized, the guy in the back only gains a d12 cover.

If the person is actively moving or trying not to be your cover, you can’t gain the bonus for actively crouching behind them.

If you don't move, however, cover becomes more and more useless as your shooter sights in. For each shot against you where you haven't moved, you either lower your effective cover percentage by 10 or shrink your cover dice by 1 size, depending on whether the cover is static or not.

Note this also applies to shields as well, so if you don't move while using a shield, it becomes less effective.

RANGED MISSES

On a miss with a ranged weapon, the missile doesn’t magically disappear into oblivion. There’s a good chance it might hit someone else, especially if your target is locked in melee.

In the case of a miss, roll a d20! and a d4. The d4 determines to which side the missile strays - an odd result means it goes left, an even result means it goes right.

The d20! determines how many feet it strays from the target. Draw an imaginary line between the archer and that point. Everyone within 5ft of that line needs to roll a defense vs the original attack roll, starting with the person closest to the archer and ending with the furthest possible target.

Inanimate objects may provide enough cover to be considered targets in both Firing into Combat and Ranged Misses, such as a large boulder or tree. In which case, medium sized objects roll a d12!, with each size lower decreasing the dice size by 2, and each size larger increasing the dice size by 2.

FIRING INTO MELEE

If there’s any one within 5 ft of a direct line between the origin of the missile and the target, they’ll have to roll the appropriate defense die vs the original attack roll to see if the missile hits them instead, starting with the person closest to the archer or gunner and ending with the intended target.

All the normal rolls apply so if the missile only misses because of cover, the missile has lodged itself in the cover and goes no further.

DEALING DAMAGE

Once both sides have rolled and added all modifiers, compare the totals against each other.

If the defender’s is equal to or greater than the attacker, the attack misses.

PARRYING If a melee attack roll is within the Parry Threshold of the defender’s total, the defender may choose to deflect some of the incoming attack.

The attacker rolls their damage and subtracts the shield or weapon’s DR and then their armor’s DR. Any leftover is still done to the defender. For piercing weapons, the /d DR of the shield or weapon is tripled.

So, a defender with a Parry Threshold of 4, and a total of 10 for their defense roll vs an attack roll of 14, could choose to parry the blow, taking less damage.

However, if it’s not, the attack lands and the attacker rolls his weapon’s damage die and adds any appropriate damage modifiers. The defender than subtracts their Damage Reduction and the remaining total is subtracted from their Vitality and added to their list of wounds.

DAMAGE REDUCTION Armor allows you to reduce the amount of damage taken. This is often times shorted to DR. There are two types: Static, noted by just a number, and Per Die, noted by a number /D.

Static simply subtracts that number from the damage and is done.

/D subtracts that number multiplied by the number of dice the attack is using. So, if someone is attacking you with a slashing weapon (2d6!), and you have armor that gives you 4/D, you’d subtract 8 from the damage.

Either way, if the attacker chooses to remain in engaged, he then adds his weapon’s Recovery to the current second on the Clock. Once the Clock reaches that new number, he may attack again.

BREAKAGE; WEAPON, ARMOR, & SHIELDS

Shield And Weapon Breakage
TypeThreshold
20
Shields & Armor30
Other Weapons40

When you parry with a shield or weapon, or when your armor absorbs damage, if the damage after DR is greater than a certain threshold, the item gains 1 Wear, which leads to a greater chance of it breaking.

If it doubles or triples this threshold, you gain double and triple the Wear, and so on.

More on Wear can be found in Chapter 10: Equipment.

KNOCK-BACKS

If the pre-DR damage is higher than your enemy’s Vitality Size Modifier + 1 for every M item you're carrying, the attack also moves them back 5 feet. If it’s twice the damage needed, they get knocked back 10 feet, and so on.

VITALITY & DAMAGE

Remember that if you drop a Wound Category you could become Fatigued.

TRAUMA

If someone does more damage after DR than your Trauma Threshold, that triggers a Con Check vs d20! + the damage dealt.

Failure means that your character has been overcome with pain to the point that he’s unable to act, leaving him completely defenseless for a number of seconds equal to how much he failed by times 3.

Double Trauma

A character that gets dealt their full Vitalty in damage in a single blow has suffered a grievous wound - the scar of which, if he survives, will be with him until the day he dies.

When this happens, the trauma is too much and they simply die where they stand if they fail their check. Even if they succeed, they’re still suffer Trauma for the next 30 seconds but gain a wicked looking scar.

Battle scars are marks of pride and honor. Characters who become scared gain a 3d4 Trait “Battle Scars”. If they would get this Trait again, increase all dice by 1 size.

COUP de GRACE

A helpless enemy is one completely at your mercy. If a character is completely helpless, either from failing a Trauma check, being bound, or deep asleep, a character can kill the target instantly. This move takes 10 seconds to complete as it takes a bit of positioning to pull off. The character’s whose throat is being slit gives out a cry of pain as they expire.

FACING & FIGHTING MULTIPLE PEOPLE

The likelihood is that at some point you’ll want to be flanking or someone with want to flank you, to try and gain an advantage.

Defending right in front of you is pretty easy and so brooks no penalties, however, every 45 degrees off that the defender lowers their Defense dice by 1 size, with those being attacked from directly behind defend on a d8! since they take advantage of a huge blind spot.

This penalty can be mitigated through the use of shields. Small shields allow you to defend against an additional assailant, depriving them of that bonus, while Medium and Large ones allow you to defend against an extra 2 and 3, respectively.

A maximum of 8 people at a time can effectively attack person at once, assuming that they’re out in the open. After that, every assailant’s attack die is lowered by 1 size per extra person.

TERRAIN & SITUATIONAL FACTORS

No battlefield is absolutely flat and featureless and often these features can make a difference for those cunning enough to use them. This section won’t give you all the different situations you might find yourself in, but should give you a good basis to judge off of.

The numbers under the Attack and Defense columns are the change penalty or bonus. If there is a dice noted there, you replace your d20! with that when rolling. So, standing in quicksand gives you a -8 to Defense (amongst other things).

Note that defense penalties also affect ranged defenses.

TACTICS

These rules add another layer of strategy to combat and should be implemented as soon as you have the basics down.

A combatant utilizing these moves can only commit to one at a time, unless otherwise stated and can only switch on their attack unless it says otherwise.

ACTIVE DODGING This move relays on actively focusing on dodging against incoming attacks.

A defender commits to how much he wants to focus on dodging incoming attacks, both melee and ranged, choosing from a bonus to his defense in the range of +1 to +5, but netting an equal penalty to his attacks (-1 to -5).

Additionally, this lowers the range for Near Perfect Defenses by 1, so you score them on a base natural 18 - 19. This tactic needs to be committed to for at least your full weapon Recovery. After that you may continue using it and change tactics at any point.

But focusing on defense does slow you down, since you're zigzagging and trying to juke out your enemy, so your movement is considered 1 Category lower for how far you move. So, if you're Medium sized and Active Dodging while at a Run, you would move at 10 ft / sec, instead of 15. You would still be subject to the damage, however, since you're still technically running.

Active Dodging can be combined with Retreat and Step Back, adding only the attack penalties but gaining all auxiliary benefits from each (so no, the defenses don't stack).

BRACE YOURSELF Maybe you look at your opponent and say "No, you move". You root your stance and ready yourself, sacrificing some maneuverability to hold the line and get a better chance of striking first.

While using this Tactic, a character adds +15 to their Knock-Back Threshold and gains a +1 to their Measure, however, they defend on a d12!.

CALLED SHOT By carefully aiming your shot, a character can reduce the effects of heavy armor by taking more time to target gaps. When employing this tactic, a character halves their effective number of damage dice (rounded up) for considering /D DR, however, it takes a bit longer to set up the strike and so they increase their Recovery by 3 seconds.

Crushing weapons are unable to employ this tactic.

CAREFUL AIMING By taking a moment or two, you can sight in with any ranged weapon, giving you a better chance to hit your target, however, there are diminishing returns after a bit.

For the first 3 seconds, you gain an increasing bonus of +1 to hit. So, the first second you’re aiming, you get a +1, the next a +2 (for a total of +3), and the third you get a +3 (for a total of +6).

After that, that bonus shrinks by 1 each second. So, the fourth second you get a +2 (for a total of +8) and the fifth second you get a +1 (for a total of +9).

Further delay gives you no bonuses.

CHARGE A character moving at least a Run can throw their full force behind a blow, knocking their opponent off their guard. If the Attacker has reach, the damage for their next attack is considered doubled for the purposes of determining Knock-Backs, though they defend with a d12! for half their weapon Recovery.

A charge is a terrifying tactic: if the blow lands, the defender takes an amount of Stress equal to how much they were hit by. If they're able to retaliate while the attacker is defending with that d12!, the attack also takes that much Stress.

CHOKING UP ON YOUR WEAPON When you’ve choked up on your weapon, your weapon’s Measure is halved, and you add that number, rounded down to its Recovery. Your halved Measure is considered your new Measure for determining who attacks first and what Tactics can be employed on you as well.

However, if an opponent is In Your Guard and you choke up on your weapon, you defend with a standard 1d20! and deal full weapon damage.

You’re unable to employ this tactic twice if someone again gets within your guard.

FEINT When performing this move, you (hopefully) get your enemy to commit his guard to a different location than you plan on hitting.

Before you roll, if you declare a Feint, you receive a +5 to that Attack but receive a -3 to Defense until your next attack.

FEND OFF If your weapon's measure is at least 1 foot greater than your opponent's, you can try and fend them off. This means that you may attack them freely, while they can't do the same to you.

You may only use this Tactic as someone is going from Threatened to Engaged, in which case they're automatically considered to be Fended Off. To full becoming engaged with you, they'll have to knock your weapon aside with a regular attack that deals no damage. Should they hit, they become fully engaged and can attack freely again.

IN THEIR GUARD If you're engaged with a person and have a weapon that is at least 1 foot shorter than your opponent, you may attempt to get in their guard. On a successful attack that deals no damage, the shorter weapon fighter has successfully knocked his foe's weapon aside and is no fighting at extreme close range, cause them huge penalties.

The longer weapon may only defend with a d12!. All damage dice is also reduced by one size (3d4! → 3d3!). They may, in turn, employ the Choke Up on Your Weapon Tactic.

FLURRY of BLOWS This tactic involves releasing a rain of quick attacks that rely more on speed than accuracy or power.

While employing this tactic, you attack 3 seconds quicker, however, you suffer a -3 to Attack, your damage doesn’t explode, and you gain 1 Stress for every 2 seconds you keep up your onslaught.

JAB A strike that is meant to purposely to open your opponent’s guard and get them off their balance by making them shift their defense.

The Jab attack happens at half weapon Recovery (rounded up) for Piercing and Slashing weapons. Instead of a d20!, the defender uses a d12! but on a successful hit, no damage is done, and the defender nets a -1 to their next full defense against a non-jab attack. This penalty doubles with each jab, however, it only lasts until the defender’s next full defense.

Crushing weapons may use this tactic but it’s not any faster than their regular Recovery.

LONG GUARD By placing your weapon further out in front of you and trying to line the body of the weapon with the defender’s eye line, this may allow a person with a short weapon the first strike.

While using this tactic, you increase your weapon’s measure by 1/2 ft but you reduce your damage’s largest die by 1 size.

Crushing weapons are unable to employ this tactic.

POWER ATTACK This is the fighting equivalent of baseball batting your enemy, swinging with all your might.

With this attack, you receive a -3 to your Attack and next defense but your damage increases

Piercing weapons increase 1 die by 1 size. Slashing weapons increase all dice by 1 size and crushing weapons increase its highest die by 2 sizes.

RETREAT This is a defensive tactic that involves moving directly back at jog or at a 45 degrees angle from your opponent’s blows at a walk.

After your defense roll, if you declare a Retreat, you receive a +5 to your next Defense roll but also a -5 to your next Attack and must continue to move back until your next attack, either at a jog, if moving directly back, or at a walk, if started to move at a 45 degrees angle.

If your opponent is using the Flurry of Blows, Feint, or the Power Attack tactic, if they do hit, you only receive half damage

SHIFT You move at your walking speed to either side of your opponent but suffer a -1 to your next Attack and Defense for every 5 ft you travel before your next Attack or Defense.

However, if you Shift with another tactic, you don’t receive this penalty and gain a +2 that can be either used to increase a bonus or decrease a penalty from that tactic. You may do this freely with any Tactic except Retreat and Step Back.

Shifting is also used to change Facings so if you’re engaged with an enemy and you Shift. And don’t forget that you get those tasty bonuses from flanking your enemy if they don’t also Shift to face you.

STEP BACK This is a defensive tactic that involves moving directly back from your opponent’s blows at your walking speed.

Before your defense roll, if you declare a Step Back, you receive a +5 to your next Defense roll but on your next attack roll shrink the damage of your dice based on its type and must continue to move back at a Walking speed until your next attack.

Piercing weapons lower 1 of their dice by 1 size. Slashing lowers all dice by 1 size and crushing lowers their highest die by 2 sizes.

If your opponent is using the Flurry of Blows, Faint, or the Power Attack tactic, if they do hit, you only receive half damage.

Retreat v. Step Back

While they may seem similar on the surface, these two different tactics have some key differences I thought I’d point out so that you can utilize each better.

1. Step Back must be declared before you roll your defense while Retreat can be declared after, meaning that if you see you rolled low before employing them.

2. Step Back moves your character straight back, while Retreat allows you to move up to 45° to the left and right, meaning that if you’re going to be backed against a wall, it’s an option to try and skirt your opponent.

3. Retreat may be done at a jogging speed, while Step Back is always at a walk.

STRIKE to BREAK This has two purposes: to either knock an opponent's weapon aside to delay their attack or to try and shatter your opponent’s weapon/shield/armor.

The defender rolls a standard defense die vs the attacker’s regular attack roll.

Should the attack succeed, he delays the defender’s next attack by a number of seconds equal to 1 for every 2 full points he scored above the defender’s total.

The attacker also gains a bonus to damaging what they're aiming for: half the breakage threshold for that item. If the damage is greater than triple this modified breakage threshold, the weapon automatically breaks.

UNARMED COMBAT

Unarmed Combat’s primary use is to either disable or control your foe, thus winning the battle by maneuvering, rather than raw damage output.

To use an unarmed attack, roll an attack on a d10!, adding your normal attack modifiers, but also adding your Damage Modifier. Your opponent rolls a regular defense (including shield unless otherwise stated) but also adds their Damage Modifier as well.

Size does count for something: the larger person, if there is one, gains a +6 for each Size Category they have on their opponent.

If you hit, you successfully pulled off the move, dealing your unarmed damage and gaining the benefit of the techinque.

If the difference between the attack and defense totals were more than the target’s Vitality Size Modifier, you may gain additional benefits listed in the move. If the difference is double the Target’s Vitality Size Modifier, they may immediately make a second unarmed combat attack for free the same second.

If you’re doing the move or defending with two hands, roll your attack on a d20!.

GRAB This move is meant to disable or gain control of a limb or item, whether that’s their belt, their leg, or their sword. The target must be declared before the roll.

Attempting to grab a shield means the target adds their shield’s unmodified Defense penalty from their roll as well since a larger shield is easier to grab. Additionally, attempting to grab most parts of the body involve grabbing armor, if they're wearing any. Add your target's armor's Skill Adjustment as well as applicable.

The exact bonus will depend on the situation but here are some guidelines:

  • WEAPON The defender gets a free attack if the move isn’t successful but when grabbed it means the defender can no longer attack.
  • SHIELD The defender no longer gains any bonus from their shield but still has the defense and Fatigue penalties.
  • ARM See Weapon if it’s the weapon’s arm and Shield if it’s the shield’s arm. The defender also defends on a d12 vs Pushes and can’t change their facing willingly.
  • LEG Moving above a crawl is impossible and moving at all can only be done after a successful opposed Str check vs d20! + the grabber’s strength.

A second grab on a non-body part works as a disarm, wrenching the item free unless secured by something strong, though at the very least, the thing is no longer in hand. This is also done on an additional benefit from a well executed move if the attacker wishes.

PUSH By pushing you opponent in just the right way, you can move them or shift them in the direction you wish.

On a successful Push, your target changes one facing or moves 5 ft to the left, right, or directly back. You may immediately Shift to follow them without penalty.

You gain an additional facing change or 5 ft of movement on a well-executed Push.

THROW/TACKLE This move attempts to throw your opponent to the ground and is easier to pull of if you go prone with them.

For this move, you can increase the attack dice size by 1 if you’re at running speed when you Tackle them. You may increase it by a further 1 if you choose to go to the ground with them, however, no matter what, if you lose the struggle, you still end up on the ground.

Getting forced to the ground resets your opponent's Count and they're considered prone.

The extra bonus for this move is being able to remain standing if you so choose, throw them 5 ft or, possibly disarming them if they fail a Str check vs d20! + your Str.

STRIKE A strike is a punch with any part of your body or shield. It’s not really about doing damage but more about throwing your opponent off.

On a successful Strike, you delay your opponent’s next attack by 1d4 seconds, though if you’re using a shield to strike, you subtract that shield’s Recovery Mod from that amount, delaying them further. Striking an already stunned opponent is less effective and so the number of second they would be further delayed is lowered by 1 for each time you've successfully struck them since their last attack, down to 0.

The extra bonus for this move is that it increases the time to a d6!.

BREAK FREE There will probably come a time when you want to break free from a grab, pin, or choke hold.

This is done through the normal unarmed combat resolution method with the following notes:

The first is that the defender rolls a d12! while you roll a full d20! as a base. Additionally, the defender adds their Rank in Escape Artist to the check.

The second is that you can only attempt this maneuver every 10 seconds, though this is modified by any Advanced Weapon Basic Training you have in unarmed attack.

MOUNTED COMBAT

Whether you're playing a knight or a bounty hunter, you might find it's to your advantage to find yourself something to ride on and you'll probably want to know how you can use that mount in combat, so here are some rules to help you out.

So you’re not making constant Skill Checks all the time, Riding, in this case, is handled a little bit differently. Your mount will have a Turning, Acceleration, and Deceleration stat: this tells you how long, at base, to perform the corresponding action. You can lower all of these base times by up to your Riding Skill, however, you can never go below 3 seconds.

You can also never perform more than one action at a time and you can’t change actions until the last one you declared is finished since your mount has its own inertia.

TURNING This is the base time it takes to turn one Facing (45 degrees). This is, however, affected by your speed, adding 1 second to the base time for every Speed Category above Walking your mount is going.

ACCELERATION/DECELERATION This moves your mount up or down 1 Speed Category, respectively.

DOING SOMETHING DANGEROUS Your mount might also be smarter than you and decide that it really doesn't want to put itself in danger. This could be charging into a wall of pikes or just a wall. In this case, you’ll have to convince your mount into taking the action.

This acts exactly like a Morale Conflict with the rider adding his Riding Skill as an additional dice and the mount adding its Bravery. Note that if the Bravery is a negative dice, it gets added to the oppositions roll.

And finally, the opposition will also add dice equal to how much damage if could do to the mount (not including DR), though if it’s non-piercing, you’re going to half the damage dice.

Since this is an Appeal, Stress is still given out to both rider and mount.

Rider Benefits
SpeedDefense BonusKB Bonus
Standing/Strolling+0 (+0)+0
Walking+2 (+4)+5
Jogging+4 (+8)+10
Running+6 (+12)+15
Sprinting+8 (+16)+20

RIDER BENEFITS So, if you have to think further ahead in combat so that you don't run into anything, what's the point of using a mount? 1) because it's awesome and 2) you get a lot of extra bonuses for doing so.

For starters, if you get +d4! to damage if your opponent isn't mounted or equalizing your height somehow, as well as additional damage dice if you're using a weapon specifically meant to be used from horseback (though you get this die even if you're on a camel, lizard, or what-have you), regardless of height difference.

On top of this, based on the Speed you're moving, you get a bonus to your Defense and bonus to your effective damage when considering Knock-Back. Should you gain a successful double Knock-Back, your opponent is trampled under hoof, dealing d8! damage and allowing you to continue past them. Note that bonuses to Defense are doubled against ranged attacks, which are the values in parenthesis for ease of reference.

Rider Penalties
SpeedAttack Dice SizeConverted (Melee/Ranged)
Standing/Strolling-0/-0d20!/d20!
Walking-1/-2d12!/d10!
Jogging-2/-4d10!/d6!
Running-3/-6d8!/0
Sprinting-4/-8d6!/-d6!

RIDER PENALTIES But, of course, it isn't all good news: moving at higher speeds means that it's harder to hit things, especially in ranged combat.

For each Speed Category above Strolling, you lower your Attack Dice by 1 size for melee and 2 sizes for ranged. This can bring you below a d4! and into the negative so lowering 1 size down from a d4! means you roll a d0 and stepping 2 sizes down gives you a -d4! to your attack. A handy table has been provided.

Remember that your Riding Skill Rank does offset this, giving you a +1 to your Attack Dice size for each Rank, which can bring it up past the standard d20!.

DYING

A character who goes passed Critical is dead. The character has died, and the player needs to grab a fresh character sheet and start rolling.

UNLESS their character falls under either of two conditions: the combat they’re in directly relates to one of their Goals or they have a Devotion related to a still living character.

In this case, they’re grievously wounded but still up and fighting: the drive to continue their goal being the only thing keeping them alive.

They can still do everything that any other character can do except that their Stress penalties now apply to their attack and not defense rolls.

If they complete their Goal, the person/thing keeping them fighting has left/died/no longer applies, the combat is over, and/or fail a Trauma check, they then die: there’s no saving them with First Aid, Medicine, or prayers.

Even if healing could be administered during or after battle through any means, it would not help the grievously wounded character: they’re a dead man walking.

FALLEN COMRADES Watching your friends die around you is extremely Stressful, even if your winning. When a side loses a quarter of their original number (either through dying, going unconscious, or running away), those remaining gain d10! Stress.

Morale

Combat is not simply about who can do the most damage the quickest. Battles, at their heart, are manifestations of the fever of conflict going on between the two sides. This is why sometimes a small, untrained force of zealots can overcome a larger professional army.

This can be represented by a Confrontation happening during the combat, or a Morale Confrontation.

MORALE CONFRONTATIONS

At the beginning of combat, if at least one side has a captain, a Confrontation is also started with the two opposing sides are the two opposing forces (the players vs the goblins, for example).

This runs like a normal Confrontation except the company captains are in charge and get to dictate pretty much everything. So as the battle progresses, the captains are going to run the Confrontation in parallel with it, trying to swing the battlefield to their advantage.

Unlike a regular Confrontation, however, it is not about gaining points but about who wins or loses that round.

If you win the Confrontation round, the target behaves the way you want. This represents either them being too panicked or not having enough information to make a good decision, or effective leadership and a clear grasp of what’s going on.

WITH A CAPTAIN

During combat, a captain can, at any time, state that they want a fighter (or group of fighters) to perform an action or behave in a certain way.

For instance, the captain might want a fighter to switch his Tactic to be more aggressive, or swap who he’s aiming for.

He can also do this for enemy fighters: the only exception is they can’t make them run away (which can happen but we'll talk about that a bit later).

A captain can affect any number of fighters, however, they need to be easily “grouped”: there needs to be a good reason why every fighter “targeted” would be affected by the same Appeal.

Stress is given out to each person depending on the number of dice they contributed to their particular side. The winning side still halves their Stress gain.

FIELD PROMOTIONS & NO CHARISMA There might be the case a captain dies and a lieutenant is suddenly promoted, however the chain of command should have been determined beforehand.

In this case, decrement any Confrontation related dice by 1 size.

If that means the new captain has no Charisma or other skills, they’ll use their Warfare skill rounded up (so a d4! by default). So, it's better than nothing because it keeps people from just running for it and they gain less Stress but still not great.

Note that this can also be used in cases where uncharismatic creatures have a captain (such as a goblin leading goblins).

A SIDE WITH NO CAPTAIN

If one side has a captain but they're removed from combat for any reason (including going unconscious) with no replacement, everyone on their side immediately has their Panic Threshold lowered by 1 Wound Category, so keep the company captain safe!

A side with no captain can’t actively go “on the attack”, meaning that they can no longer initiate a Confrontation round but only defend against them.

If they don't have any applicable personal Confrontation Die, they auto fail and gain double the Stress the enemy captain takes.

Additionally, sides with no captains are now vulnerable to having their fighters break and run away.

RUNNING AWAY Normally, running away is only caused by a fighter reaching their Stress Threshold, but, with no captain, any Panicked fighter can be convinced to turn tail and run, inflicting any Stress it would gain on the remaining fighters.

THE PERSONAL CONFRONTATION

Whether or not you have a captain, individual fighters can also initiate their own, personal Confrontations with those they’re fighting.

These are typically 1 round affairs (or treated as such), and only involve the fighters’ Characteristics and any situational arguments that apply.

ADDITIONAL ARGUMENTS

On top of that you’ll also have additional places you can pull from for your arguments that only really apply to Morale Confrontations, such as having a seasoned commander, outnumbering the opposing side, having a common Devotion to why they’re fighting and so on.

Here are common things you can draw from:

OUTNUMBERING THE ENEMY Each side can add their total numbers to an Appeal, thus favoring the side with the most people.

CATCHING AN ENEMY BY SURPRISE This nets you a d8 until their initiative comes up.

HAVING YOU BATTLE STANDARD UP AND VISIBLE Nets you a d8.

CAUSING AN ENEMY TO PANIC Nets you a d6.

CAUSING AN ENEMY TO FLEE Nets you a d10. This can increase the amount of dice you gain from outnumbering your enemy.

PARTY CAPTAIN’S CHARISMA You also gain your party captain’s Confrontation Dice from Charisma in this case. Also, any abilities that the Party Captain has that effect Confrontations can also be used during this Confrontation.

A PERFECT ATTACKS & DEFENSE this nets you a d6! until that person’s next Attack or Defense.

Note that if the argument is based on circumstance, like having an enemy Panic, it goes away when that circumstance does.

DEVOTIONS Normally, Devotions would add to your points automatically but since points don’t matter, it would make them useless in Morale Confrontations but, as we all know, why you fight does matter. If a Devotion applies to all the people being targeted that round, they add the lowest applicable Devotion to each dice. So with a Devotion of 1, if you're rolling 3d4, you'd get a +3.

Who Controls the Pool

It may be the case that the Party Leader, who should be responsible for the Morale Dice Pool is also your main line fighter and is already busy juggling an enemy or two.

You can have a player who runs a less combat intense character, such as the magic user or diplomat, be in charge of the Morale Dice Pool in that case and decide where to risk dice, however, only the actual Party Leader’s abilities affect the number or size of those dice applied.

Ending Combat

Combat ends when one side is incapacitated, reached a truce, or have fled the field. At that point the Clock stops, and play moves back to narrative time.

If you engaged in a Morale Confrontation, everyone on the side that won the combat gains the difference in winning pools to their Honor, regardless of whether or not their side has less. So, if the players has 3 in their winning pool and the goblins had 7, every player still gains 4 Honor.

The losing side subtracts that with the same caveats.

Trainings

CALCULATING BASELINE COMBAT ABILITIES

Before we get into trainings and all that jazz, let's calculate your baseline abilities. This is from the corresponding stat as laid out in Chapter 12: Stats but here is a quick summary.

An (A) or (S) means that these abilities will be affected by your Armor and/or Shield, if you're wearing them.

  • Attack Dex Mod + Int Mod
  • Defense Dex Mod + Wis Mod + A + S
  • Initiative Dex Mod + Wis Mod + A
  • Damage Str Mod

You'll also have Parry and Recovery, which are both based on the weapon you used but Parry may be replaced by your shield's Parry while your Recovery will be affected by your Armor.

ARMOR BASIC TRAINING

This basic training allows a character to more effectively utilize armor of different types based on their level of familiarity with them. It’s split into 3 categories:

LIGHT (15 CrP) This covers armor that tends to not to restrict the wearer so is often favored by skill centric characters.

MEDIUM (10 CrP) This armor is a bit heavier than Light armor and offers a tradeoff between protection and mobility.

HEAVY (10 CrP) The armor in this category is durable, thick, and heavy, able to withstand blows but at the cost of being hard to move in.

You have to buy armor training in sequence, starting at Light and moving all the way to Heavy.

You can always try and use armor without the baseline knowledge of how to properly wear and don it, but the Defense penalty is doubled and you're always considered Fatigued if that’s the case.

You can further your training in armor to increase the benefits. See the Weapon, Shield, & Armor Advanced Training Sidebar for more details.

SHIELD BASIC TRAINING

For 10 CrP, this teaches a character the basic understanding of how to properly use and employ shields in combat. This training covers all types of shields.

You can always try to use a shield without the training; however, you gain a -4 to your defense when doing so.

You can further your training in shields to increase the benefits. See the Weapon, Shield, & Armor Advanced Training Sidebar for more details.

WEAPON BASIC TRAINING

By gaining the basic understanding of how a weapon is wielded, used and taken care of, you not only expand its longevity, but also your longevity on the battlefield.

Weapon training costs 10 CrP and is taken for individual weapons, however its benefits extend to weapons of that share similar features.

A person completely untrained in weapons both Attacks and Defends with that weapon using a d12!.

However, one trained uses a d20! Attack and Defense (depending on whether or not they use a shield).

Additionally, this is extended to any weapon that shares similar qualities.

A character with training in both a Melee and Ranged weapons, gains that training to all weapons.

So, why would you take training in multiple weapons once you have one melee and one ranged? Because this training allows you to further master that weapon, increasing its usefulness.

You can further your training in weapons to increase the benefits. See the Weapon, Shield, & Armor Advanced Training Sidebar for more details.

Minimum Weapon Recovery

How fast a weapon can move and maneuver is ultimately limited by its size and balance and no matter how much you train, you can’t overcome that hurtle.

Small Melee Weapons: 2 Seconds

Medium Melee Weapons: 3 Seconds

Large Melee Weapons: 4 Seconds

Small Ranged Weapons: 3 Seconds

Medium Ranged Weapons: 4 Seconds

Large Ranged Weapons: 5 Seconds

Weapon, Shield, & Armor Advanced Training

Weapon, Shield, & Armor Advanced Trainings are further training in those particular fields, like the name implies. They can only be taken for things that you already have Basic Training in.

Each of these types of Training has two things: Tiers, which represent how broad your knowledge is (sort of like Suite Suites and Advanced Skills), and Ranks.

Every type will also have areas you can specialize in. These are the same across the Tiers of that type of Advanced Training, but you train in specific Tiers. So, Armor Advanced Training has Defense, Recovery Modifier, Fatigue, and Initiative as its areas. Each purchase in that area gives them a Rank in one of the Tiers, which is a bonus that’s applied when applicable. This bonus can't bring a negative bonus above 0, however.

The price for increasing your Rank in an area is that Tier’s Cost + twice the current Rank.

Rank 1Rank 2Rank 3Rank 4Rank 5
DefXX
InitXXXX
RecoveryXXX
FatigueXXXXX

You can’t buy up a Rank again for a given Tier until you’ve either filled out all the rest of the areas for that Tier or you have two areas in that Tier at the current highest Rank. This is true all the way down so your Advanced Training forms a weird looking triangle (as shown to the left). This is true even if you have reached the maximum benefit from an area.

Tiers don't stack with each other: you gain all the bonuses from a single Tier of your choice. If you have a +1 Attack and Damage in all melee weapons and a +1 to Defense and -1 to Recovery in polearms, while wielding a pike, you either gain the +1 Attack and Damage with it or +1 Defense and -1 Recovery, but not +1 Attack, Damage, Defense, and -1 Recovery.

Bonuses cascade down through tiers but only if they’re applicable.

COST

The Base Costs are listed next to each tier. The number in parenthesis is what Fighter Archetypes pay instead BEFORE any Improved Advanced Training Archetype Abilities they may have.

ARMOR

Tier 3: All Armor (including improvised armor)

Tier 2: Armor Type (Light, Medium, Heavy)

Tier 1: Specific Armor (Gambeson, Scale, Maile, etc.)

AREAS Defense, Recovery Modifier, Fatigue, Initiative

Fatigue lowers that armor's Fatigue by 1 Category for every 2 Ranks you have in it.

SHIELDS

Tier 3: All Shield (including improvised shields)

Tier 2: Shield Size (Small, Medium, Medium)

Tier 1: Specific Shield (Buckler, Kite, Round, etc.)

AREAS Defense, Parry, Fatigue, Breakage

Fatigue lowers that shield's Fatigue by 1 Category for every 2 Ranks you have in it.

Breakage increases each Shield Breakage Threshold by 2 points for every rank obtained. So, wielding a shield with 2 ranks in this area of training means that your shield can take 19 points of damage before saving vs d20!+10, 24 before saving vs d20!, and 29 before saving vs d20!-10. This training doesn't affect weapon breakage.

MELEE WEAPONS

Tier 3: All Melee Weapons (including improvised melee weapons)

Tier 2: Weapon Type (Sword, Polearm, Trauma, etc.)

Tier 1: Specific Weapon (Arming Sword, Handaxe, Mace, etc.)

AREAS Attack, Damage, Parry, Recovery

RANGED WEAPONS

Tier 3: All Ranged Weapons (including improvised ranged weapons)

Tier 2: Firing Type (Thrown, Mechanical, Firearm)

Tier 1: Specific Weapon (Sling, Longbow, Throwing Axe, etc.)

AREAS Attack, Damage, Recovery

UNARMED COMBAT ADVANCED TRAINING

While you can gain Advanced Trainings in Unarmed like other weapons, because it’s not treated exactly like a weapon, you’ll have different areas in which you can become specialized in it. It is also treated as Tier 2 with costs of 12 and 10 respectively.

AREAS Attack, Defense, Recovery, Effectiveness

Effectiveness is applied to the difference between the Attack and Defense for additional benefits ONLY if the attack hits. Every purchase adds +3 times the Tier to the total. So, Tier 3 in Effectiveness gives you a +9 to your total.

Additionally, regardless of the number of Advanced Trainings in Unarmed Combat, you’re never treated as if you have a weapon.

Ranged Weapon Firing Break Down

If you’re wondering what you’re doing at any given second when firing a ranged weapon: here’s a breakdown for you.

So, if you already loading and cranked (drawing) your crossbow, it would only take 5 seconds to fire it.

MISSILE RETRIEVAL This is drawing the missile from its quiver or pouch. For firearms, that means drawing out the powder and bullets.

FUSE This is the process of lighting the fuse. This can be dropped to 1 second but increases the chance of backfiring from 1 to 5.

LOADING This is knocking the arrow or loading the bullet and powder in the barrel.

DRAW This is drawing back the bow, cranking the crossbow, or cocking your arm for the throw. This can be prepped ahead of time for the belly- and crossbows but not the composite, long, or war bows

AIMING This is actually sighting your target and lining up the shot. This can be skipped, making it so you fire faster, but you roll attack on a d12! instead of the standard d20!. This can't bring a weapon below its minimum weapon Recovery.

FIRING This is pulling the trigger or loosing the arrow.

Advanced Training that lowers Recovery take from Missile Retrieval, Fuse, Loading, Drawing, and Aiming evenly but they can never reduce a single category to below 1 second. So, the minimum firing Recovery for a Composite Bow is 5 seconds.

Equipment

Now it’s time to kit out your character and get them suited up to hit the road.

Most things won’t warrant an explanation, but some have been provided for further clarification.

Carry

You’ve probably noticed that Carry under your Strength Stat is just a string of numbers and letters and you probably wondered: what does that mean? Well, I’m about to tell you.

Instead of each item having a weight in pounds or ounces, each item is assigned a size that represents, not only how much it weighs, but also its general bulky and awkwardness. These sizes correspond to the naming conventions set up in Chapter 14: Misc Rules however, just because something is Medium sized here, doesn’t mean it’s the size of a human - again, item sizes are based on their weights, bulkiness, and awkwardness to carry.

Your Carry matches up with that, so if you have 2M as your Carry, you can carry 2 Medium items - or 2M items.

You can also swap out 1 for 3 of the size lower. So instead of carrying 2M items, you could carry 1M item and 3S items.

Each individual instance of an item counts towards this: carrying two daggers, both S items, would count as 2S for Carry.

WEARING CLOTHES, ARMOR, & PERSONAL CONTAINERS

Normally what is written under the Carry column for each item is its Size. The only exception is clothes, armor, and personal containers. If worn on your person, not carried or stuffed in a backpack, these become less encumbering since their weight is distributed across basically the entire body.

In this case, shrink them 1 size for Carry purposes. Small items become negligible. Wearing boots, a Small item, then makes them effectively weightless, while any Heavy Armor makes them only M.

PERSONAL CONTAINERS

Personal Containers, such as backpacks or sacks, go one step further. Since it is assumed that your Carry weight is how much you can hold effectively, it does take into account that some things are too awkward to effectively carry despite being fairly light.

These containers increase your Carry by the noted amount.

The exceptions, sort of, to this are coin purses, which make coins negligible (otherwise they’re 10 = 1S), quivers which do the same thing but with arrows, and scabbards and sheaths allow you to wear weapons - shrinking them 1 size.

GOING OVER CARRY

You can go over your Carry, of course, but it will slow you down. Going over your Carry prevents you from Sprinting.

Beyond that, each M of weight over your limit removes the next quickest Movement Category (so, removing Running, Jogging, Walking, and then Crawling) until your character is unable to move.

Unless proven otherwise, the GM should assume the characters are carrying their max Carry if they pick up something new while out adventuring.

Currency

The denomination of coinage is fairly simple and is done in divisions of 10.

10 cc = 1 silver coin (sc)

10 sc = 1 gold coin (gc)

10 gc = 1 platinum coin (pc)

So, 100 cc is equal to 1 gc.

The silver coin is the base of the economy and is what most day-to-day interactions use. If a denomination isn’t noted, it’s assumed to be sc.

Note that 100 coins is 1S

Buying At 1st Level

A character starts with one of the following kits. After doing so, he rolls the kit's Extra Cash dice to determine any extra starting money to buy additional gear. When buying new items, use the Source price.

The individual sizes are notes next to the item. Note that anything with * at the end denotes that the item is worn and so the size has been adjusted accordingly. You're also given the sellback price (in sc) in case you don't want the item. For weapons, you'll notice that you're giving a generic size of weapon you can select from - the sellback price is unaffected by which weapon you would have picked up.

Wear

Wear represents how close your weapon is to breaking.

Simply put: if, on a Skill Check, Attack roll (for weapons), or Defense roll (for shields and armor), you roll equal to or below the Wear of that item on the d20, that item breaks and becomes functionality unusable until it’s repaired.

There are some specific cases that should be noted:

1) You still maintain any penalties (Fatigue, Defense Modifiers from armor and shields, for example) that item gives you unless you discard it.

2) In the case of weapons, you can still use the broken weapon - you just half all the damage it does.

3) And finally, instead of having the item break, if it’s a Superior quality item, you can instead reduce one of its Traits by 1 dice size as long as you have at least Trait at a d4!.

REPAIRING EQUIPMENT

Since the Maintenance Skill will only get you so far, you might need to remove Wear from your gear through blacksmithing. This is done through personally having the applicable Skill (Leather- or Metalworking, for example) or through contract work.

HIRING IT OUT If you just hand off your gear to a blacksmith, the price for repairs is 10% the base Source price for the item (not factoring in the price increase for it being Superior if that's the case) per point of Wear and takes 1 day for every 2 sc the repairs cost.

DOING IT YOURSELF If personally done through the appropriate Skill, the cost for raw materials is 5% the base Source price for the item per point of Wear with the base time being 1 day for every 4 sc, however, two things to note:

The first is that you need to roll a Check vs d20! + the amount of Wear rounded to the next largest dice size (so, 4 Wear becomes a d6!) as the end of that time. Failure results in an amount of leftover Wear that will require twice the time and raw materials to fix at which point you'll need to make another Check.

The last thing to consider is that if you don't have a forge for metalworking, you'll have to rent one, which can range from 1cc to 1sc per day depending on how busy the forge (good luck trying to rent one at a military camp).

Superior Equipement

Not all equipment is built the same and, if you have some extra coin, it might benefit you to invest in some good quality gear.

Superior gear is always unique and even two +1 longswords can be different. This is done through Traits and they're like the Traits that your character has: they're used to describe the gear.

During a Confrontation, Combat, or Challenge, if that Trait applies, you can gain an extra d4! to what is going on. This is in addition to the normal d6! for gear you get in a Confrontation and the reroll that you would normal get in a Skill Challenge.

This, however, comes at a cost: when used this way in Combat or a Skill Challenge, your gear becomes a little more worn, gaining 1 Wear.

You can have additional Traits on a single weapon and/or increase the dice size of Traits you already have. Increasing the power simply increases the size of the bonus dice you get.

Every time you do so adds an extra 50% the cost of the item.

War Gear

It’s generally in poor taste to walk around town or go about your daily business armed like you're going to battle. Even nobles don’t make a habit of breaking out their plate when surveying their fief as it signals a general distrust of the smallfolk.

Wearing armor heavier than light, and weapons and armor larger than small while in town or another similarly inappropriate setting docks a person 1 Honor for each infringement at the end of session.

If they enter a Confrontation with someone while they’re clearly being aggressive, the person receives an additional 4d4! per escalation to resist their charms. This applies in the case of an armed man trying to haggle with the shop keeper but not, for instance, in the case of two nobles on the battlefield meeting in the middle to exchange terms and verbal blows with one another.

This penalty can be overcome by simply storing your war equipment on your cart, horse, or arming squire.

Appropriate Attire

These rules can really be applied to most situations. A character showing up to a noble’s banquet in rags is going to be just out of place as one in armor, if not more so. Players should be warned if their characters would know they’re walking into a situation where they might earn the derision of others by their attire.

Table Header Explanations

The following is a breakdown of how to read the following tables. Note that the prices here are not absolute and are actually the lowest price you’ll find for a give item based on the area. War, tariffs, what relationship the buyer has with the seller, and even the political climate can cause these numbers to fluctuate wildly.

Name This is the common name for the item.

Weight This is the weight of the object in pounds.

Source This is the wholesale sc cost of producing the item.

Local Market This is the sc price of purchasing this item within a day’s travel of the place where it was manufactured.

Nearby City This is the sc price of picking up this item within about a week’s travel from where the thing was crafted.

Distance City This is the sc price of the item in faraway markets, either across a political border or beyond a month’s travel away. This price increases the further away the city is, obviously.

MOUNT STATS

Each mount is different though they do have ranges based on the breed and this is represented by their unique set of stats that are rolled for each mount you look at while shopping. These can usually be discerned by a Handle Animal Check that's, of course, appropriate to the type. So, nice try, but Handle Animal (horse) won't help you figure out which riding lizard is best.

These stats are then used to determine the specifics of each mount: how fast can they get up and go? How long can they run for? Each of the main stats has a positive effect on some of these derived stats and a negative effect on others so figuring out the right balance for what you want out of a mount can be tricky. Averages for each breed are found under that breed's profile in the Bonfire Bestiary.

You’ll note the min roll for each stat at the very top of the table for easy reference.

SMALL & LARGE SIZED CREATURES

Obviously, a smaller creature requires less material to make a suite of armor while a larger one requires more, so armor built for Small-sized creatures cost half the price while armor for Large-sized creatures double it.

Armor Table Header Explanation

DR This number is subtracted from the damage dealt by a weapon.

Def All armor weighs you down and makes it harder to dodge incoming attacks. This number is added to your Defense roll.

Init Mod Armor is restricting, even at the best of times. This is added to your Initiative roll at the start of combat.

Rec Mod Again, armor is restricting. This is added to your weapon’s Recovery.

Type This donates what category your armor falls in. You should note that should you purchase Medium or Heavy armor, this comes with a Gambeson that’s worn underneath for comfort. This is already factored into the other stats of the armor.

Weight This is how heavy your armor is for Carry when your armor is carried on your person.

Fatigue This donates at what Wound Category you become Fatigued. A for Always, H for Hurt, B for Bloodied, and W for Wounded.

Skill Adj Armor, regardless of how fitted, is restricting and heavy, making it harder to perform certain skills in certain gear. This is the number applied to physical Skill Checks.

Cost per Day
ArtisanAdvanced Skill Base Cost
Mercenary5
Unskilled Laborer1

Artisans and Mercenaries starts with 3 points. These points can be spent on skills, training, or equipment. The price to increase something = bonus + (current bonus * 2), minimum 1, except equipment which is a number of points = Source price/100, rounded up.

You get to distribute a number of points equal to your Recruiting Rank - the GM gets to spend the rest.

Basic Food & Lodgings This is not normally included. +1 sc/day.

Hazard Pay Artisans and Unskilled Laborers will demand a share of the loot or double pay for anything dangerous. Mercenaries normally don't unless the job is more dangerous than you let on when you hired them.

More Skilled Artisan and Mercenaries 1 extra point per +1 sc/day they demand.

Improved Morale Doubling the pay per day gives a d4! to morale checks. Each doubling after increases it by 1 dice size.

Charisma Subtract Charisma in cc from price/day.

Shield Table Header Explanation

Size This indicates the general size of a shield. It also determines how tough your shield is.

DR This number is subtracted from the damage dealt by a weapon on a successful parry with a shield.

Def This is the number that you subtract from your character’s defense. Lugging a huge piece of material on the end of your arm makes dodging a bit difficult. Additionally, you’re often moving a shield into harm’s way, meaning, overall, you get hit more while wielding one.

Parry Some shields are balanced better than others and that allows you to deflect blows more effectively. This number is the shield’s Parry Threshold. This is overrides by your weapon’s Parry. Parrying with a shield subjects it to the Shield Breakage rules.

Cover This dice is added to your Defense roll against ranged weapons. If you’re actively crouching behind it, you instead add the maximum value of any dice and roll of that dice (i.e., a d8, you add d8 + 8, for d12 + d8, you add d12 + d8 + 20, etc.).

Flanks This is the number of additional attackers from the side that the shield can defend against. More on that is found in Chapter 10: Combat

Weight This is how heavy your shield is for your Carry.

Fatigue This number adjust the Wound Category at which you become Fatigued. So a shield with Fatigue -1 means that you become Fatigued at Wounded, instead of Critical (assuming you're not wearing armor as this penalty stacks with armor's).

Bonus Some shields have additional unique bonuses. If there is a ‘yes’ in this column, look up the shield in the following section to find out more details.

For This is a recommendation for who would benefit most from using this type of shield. "S" stands for Specialist, meaning that if you're a primarily a shield fighter, this shield will get drastically better as you take Advanced Trainings in it but it will not serve your average person well.

"W" stands for warrior: this is a solid shield for frontline fighters who are going to be specializing in other things and only lightly dipping into Shield Advanced Trainings.

"A" stands for anyone: these shields serve as cheap replacements for armor, and anyone can pick one of these up and get an instant benefit from the.

Shield Bonuses

Buckler The buckler is a lightweight shield which relies more on parrying blows than outright stopping them. The buckler also has the added benefit of being rather small with almost no weight, allowing it to be worn but not used while wielding two handed weapons. You receive no bonuses when doing this, but it means that if you do switch over to a one-handed weapon, you instantly get the buckler’s bonus.

Figure Eight This shield is designed specifically for phalanx or shield wall tactics. This gives a +2 Parry and a Cover of d4 to anyone fighting on either side of you. This stacks with any Parry or Cover that they have from either their weapon or shields.

Pavise This shield is less a shield and more portable cover employed by gunners and crossbowman, rather than fighters.

However, the Pavise can be set up using a kick stand in the back, giving anyone crouching behind it basically complete cover and allowing that person the ability to steady their weapon on it.

Round This shield was meant for hitting your opponent, trying to throw them off guard. On a natural roll of 20 on Defense or an Unarmed Strike, you delay your opponent’s next attack by 5 seconds.

Weapon Table Header Explanation

Size A weapon’s size indicates the smallest size the wielder can be to use it 1 handed. Weapons 1 size larger must be wielded with 2 hands and those 2 sizes greater must be wielded with 2 hands and the wielder gains a -2 to their Fatigue (meaning that if you would normally become Fatigued at Critical, you'd instead become Fatigued at Bloodied).

Damage This is the damage inflicted when a successful hit is scored.

Recovery This is the number of seconds a character locked in melee must wait before attacking again with this weapon.

Dam Type This tells you whether the weapon is Slashing (S), Piercing (P), or Crushing (C).

Parry Some weapons are balanced better than others and that allows you to deflect some blows. This is the weapon’s Parry Threshold. This is overridden by your shield’s Parry if you’re using one. A successful parry with a weapon has a DR of 2/D. Parrying with a weapon subjects it to the Shield Breakage rules.

Measure This is a measure in feet of how long a weapon is.

Bonus Some weapons have additional unique bonuses. If there is a ‘yes’ in this column, look up the weapon in the following section to find out more details.

Weapon Bonuses

Arming Sword Whenever you successfully parry with your arming sword, you may make a free attack with the arming sword. This free attack resets your cooldown.

Bardiche This weapon can be braced for a charge. It takes 3 seconds to do so and deals double damage to charging foes if you hit.

Bellybow All this weapon’s damage is counted as if it were on 1 dice for /D armor.

Recovery Advanced Trainings for this weapon remove 1.5 seconds (rounded down) off their Recovery per bonus instead of 1.

Bill Damage with this weapon is effectively doubled for Knock-Backs vs mounted defenders.

Bludgeon Damage with this weapon is effectively doubled for Trauma checks.

Composite Bow This is the only mechanical ranged weapon that may be used from horseback.

This weapon requires two hands regardless of size.

Court Sword When rolling damage, reroll the lowest die. It’s doesn’t explode.

Crossbow If you steady this weapon against the ground or a stand, you may double your Attack bonus from training with this weapon.

Recovery Advanced Trainings for this weapon remove 1.5 seconds (rounded down) off their Recovery per bonus instead of 1.

Dagger This weapon can be used as either a Slashing or Piercing weapon. You only need one Weapon Basic Training, and any Weapon Advanced Trainings count for both.

Estoc When wielding this weapon two handed, damage from it is effectively dealt on 1 die.

Fire Lance This weapon’s loud noise causes everyone to duck. Everyone within its range flinches for a number of seconds equal to 1 of the d10s, determined before hand (see Terrain and Situational Factors section of Chapter 9). This includes the gunner as well.

This penalty is lowered by 1 for every combat a person has been in that involves firearms or is completely negated if they have Basic Weapon Training (Any Firearm).

If several firearms have gone off on the same second, you take the highest delay rolled. Additionally, those already stunned by a firearm when another goes off are unaffected by that firearm's delay.

This is a single use weapon.

Guisarme Damage with this weapon is effectively doubled for Knock-Backs vs mounted defenders.

Halberd This weapon can be used as either a Slashing or Piercing weapon. You only need one Weapon Basic Training and any Weapon Advanced Trainings count for both.

Hakenbuchse If you steady this weapon against the ground or a stand, you may double your Attack bonus from training with this weapon.

Recovery Advanced Trainings for this weapon remove 1.5 seconds (rounded down) off their Recovery per bonus instead of 1.

This weapon also comes with a drop-down pole that takes 5 seconds to deploy or stow away.

This weapon’s loud noise causes everyone to duck. Everyone within the first two range categories of this weapon flinches for a number of seconds equal to what the smaller of its two damage dice rolls (see Terrain and Situational Factors section of Chapter 9). If they’re still surprised, they can act after those seconds are over. This includes the gunner as well.

This penalty is lowered by 1 for every combat a person has been in that involves firearms or is completely negated if they have Basic Weapon Training (Any Firearm).

If several firearms have gone off on the same second, you take the highest delay rolled. Additionally, those already stunned by a firearm when another goes off are unaffected by that firearm's delay.

This weapon Backfires (see sidebar).

Back Firing

On a roll of 1 on your attack dice, this weapon jams, making it unusable until repaired.

On top of this, you need to roll another d20. On the roll of 20, the gun still goes off but does half damage and the firing mechanism is broken so the firearm is unusable until repaired.

On the roll of a second 1, the gun backfires, dealing half damage to you. At least, it’s not jammed?

Handgonne This weapon’s loud noise causes everyone to duck. Everyone within the first two range categories of this weapon flinches for a number of seconds equal to what the smaller of its two damage dice rolls (see Terrain and Situational Factors section of Chapter 9). If they’re still surprised, they can act after those seconds are over. This includes the gunner as well.

This penalty is lowered by 1 for every combat a person has been in that involves firearms or is completely negated if they have Basic Weapon Training (Any Firearm).

If several firearms have gone off on the same second, you take the highest delay rolled. Additionally, those already stunned by a firearm when another goes off are unaffected by that firearm's delay.

This weapon Backfires (see sidebar).

Handaxe This weapon can be used either in melee or as the Throwing Axe. Tier 2 Melee Weapon and Ranged Weapon Advanced Trainings are shared

Horsemans Pick This weapon is designed for use on horseback. Double its damage dice while mounted.

Javelin If this weapon strikes a shield. and its damage is greater than the shield’s DR, the javelin has gotten lodged in the shield’s surface.

The Defender must either spend 2d6! seconds pulling it out or defend with a base 1d12! for as long as he continues to use the shield.

Unlike other ranged weapons, you add your Damage Modifier from Strength to your damage.

Knife This weapon can be used either in melee or as the Throwing Axe. Tier 2 Melee Weapon and Ranged Weapon Advanced Trainings are shared

Koncerz This weapon is designed for use on horseback. Double its damage dice while mounted.

Lance When mounted, this weapon can be wielded 1 handed without penalty.

Lochaber Axe This weapon can be braced for a charge. It takes 3 seconds to do so and deals double damage to charging foes if you hit.

Lucerne This counts as having one effective less damage dice for the purposes of /D.

The Weird

What Is Weird?

Seems like a reasonable place to start. It is called a lot of things by different cultures: mana, magic, juju, but essentially, it’s the metaphysical power that allows people to reshape reality, which is why working with it is often called weirdshaping, or simply shaping, if you don’t want to sound pretentious.

Weird lies beneath the surface of all things, a hidden power that imbues life with those indescribable feelings. It is the reason you feel attracted to someone at first sight, are speechless when you see a beautiful view, or the reason why planets have healing properties. It is not that some things are inherently ‘magical’ or ‘non-magical’ - all things have weird that is waiting to be shaped.

Anatomy Of A Spell

When you shape the weird, it manifests in the Middle Realm as a spell, which is broken up into different parts or “Rudiments”, which are split into types. More details on the specifics are found under the Acquiring Rudiments section below but here’s a brief overview:

SHAPE This is the shape that the spell shows up in. This can be a circle, cone, or wall, for example.

RANGE This depends on the Shape of the spell but generally it’s the reach of the spell: how long is the cone or how far can you launch a salvo shaped spell.

ORIGIN This is where the spell comes into being: this can start as the weird-adept and emanate out, it can imminent from his touch, or it can manifest yards away from him.

EFFECT This is where the spell usually gets its name and it’s what it actually does. A spell can cause Stress, remove friction, change appearances, and so on. Effects will also list the Interval, which will talk about next, and the base Range for that spell.

Effects are also special because you can add multiple Effects (or even the same effect multiple times, thus making it more powerful) to the same spell but increasing the Drain in the same amount. If it isn’t clear how an Effect would work if Stacked with itself, there’s a Stacking section for each Effect that should clear things up.

SHAPING A SPELL

Each of a spell’s Rudiments will have a Drain value, which added together, is how much that spell costs to cast. A spell must have at least 1 of each Rudiment type.

FALLOUT

Bending reality and shaping primordial force is not an easy thing to do and nature doesn’t like it. When you first shape a spell, you take 5 Stress +5 for every Category above Critical that your Fatigue is at (10 for Wounded, 15 for Bloodied, etc.). This is a one-time cost so continuing to shape the same spell doesn't further Stress you out (as long as you don't suffer weirdburn, of course).

You can mitigate this by refusing some of the power. Lowering the number of SP you receive lowers the Stress you would gain on a 1 to 1 basis.

Weird Traditions

Ways of shaping the Weird are split up into different “Traditions”, which with their own bent on ways the Weird can manifest. These are:

ANIMISM

This Tradition focuses on the manipulation of the body, forcing it to endure beyond its normal limits and making it into a weapon.

CALLING

This Tradition focuses on the Realms beyond the Middle one we’re so fond of. It involves the summoning of demons, elementals, and spirits.

CHARM

This focuses on the manifestation of the Weird in its raw and primal form. This allows the Weird to interact with our world in a more physical way.

GLAMOUR

This Tradition focuses on the manipulation of the senses, tricking the brain into seeing what the weird-adept wants you to see.

INVESTITURE

This focuses on inanimate objects and either imbuing them with power or manifesting the latent potential that is already in them.

OCCULTISM

This Tradition focuses on the hidden world and opening of the third mind: the divination of knowledge and the unseen.

SORTILEGE

This Tradition focuses on fate, chance, and probability: more specifically, it focuses on messing with it so that things go in your favor.

Acquiring Rudiments

At 1st level and as dedicated by your Archetypes, you’ll have the opportunity to acquire new Rudiments, which you can then use to shape spells. You’ll do so by picking a Rudiment table and then rolling on it. If you roll a Rudiment you already have, you may select the Rudiment on either side of it. For Effects, you roll on the Effects Rudiments table and then go to the corresponding sub-table. You may spend a CrP to reroll as per the norm, even during level up.

Players that seek out the knowledge to gain a specific Rudiment during play can forgo this roll and simply gain that Rudiment, however, they can’t learn more Rudiments than their Archetype and level dictates.

At first level, it is required that you have at least 1 Rudiment from each type (so, 1 Shape Rudiment, 1 Origin Rudiment, and so on) but once that’s satisfied, they can go back.

You can also purchase additional Rudiments for 15 CrP a pop.
